id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">JAXA</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Japanese_Space_Agency&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency<br /><span style="font-size:90%;"><i><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">\xe5\xae\x87\xe5\xae\x99\xe8\x88\xaa\xe7\xa9\xba\xe7\xa0\x94\xe7\xa9\xb6\xe9\x96\x8b\xe7\x99\xba\xe6\xa9\x9f\xe6\xa7\x8b</span></i></span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Jaxa_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="Jaxa logo.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Jaxa_logo.svg/220px-Jaxa_logo.svg.png" width="220" height="137" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Jaxa_logo.svg/330px-Jaxa_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Jaxa_logo.svg/440px-Jaxa_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="444" data-file-height="276" /></a><div><i>Explore to Realize</i></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Acronym</th><td>\nJAXA</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Owner</th><td>\nJapan</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Established</th><td>\n1&#160;October 2003<span class="noprint">&#59;&#32;15 years ago</span><span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2003-10-01</span>)</span><br /><i><span style="font-size:90%;">Successor agency to <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Development_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="National Space Development Agency">NASDA</a> 1969\xe2\x80\x932003, <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Space_and_Astronautical_Science" title="Institute of Space and Astronautical Science">ISAS</a> 1981\xe2\x80\x932003 and <a href="/wiki/National_Aerospace_Laboratory_of_Japan" title="National Aerospace Laboratory of Japan">NAL</a> 1955\xe2\x80\x932003</span></i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Headquarters</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dfu,_Tokyo" title="Ch\xc5\x8dfu, Tokyo">Ch\xc5\x8dfu, Tokyo</a>, Japan</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Spaceport" title="Spaceport">Primary spaceport</a></th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">Tanegashima Space Center</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Motto</th><td>\nOne JAXA</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Administrator</th><td>\nNaoki Okumura</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Budget</th><td>\n\xc2\xa5211.11 billion/ $2.03 billion (FY2013)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Website</th><td>\n<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jaxa.jp/index_e.html">www.jaxa.jp</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency</b> (<b>JAXA</b>)<span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\x9b\xbd\xe7\xab\x8b\xe7\xa0\x94\xe7\xa9\xb6\xe9\x96\x8b\xe7\x99\xba\xe6\xb3\x95\xe4\xba\xba\xe5\xae\x87\xe5\xae\x99\xe8\x88\xaa\xe7\xa9\xba\xe7\xa0\x94\xe7\xa9\xb6\xe9\x96\x8b\xe7\x99\xba\xe6\xa9\x9f\xe6\xa7\x8b</span><span style="display:none">,</span>&#32;<i>Kokuritsu-kenky\xc5\xab-kaihatsu-h\xc5\x8djin Uch\xc5\xab K\xc5\x8dk\xc5\xab Kenky\xc5\xab Kaihatsu Kik\xc5\x8d</i>, literally "National Research and Development Agency on Aerospace Research and Development")</span> is the Japanese national aerospace and <a href="/wiki/Space_agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Space agency">space agency</a>. Through the merger of three previously independent organizations, JAXA was formed on 1 October 2003. JAXA is responsible for research, technology development and launch of <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a> into <a href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth orbit">orbit</a>, and is involved in many more advanced missions such as <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> exploration and possible manned <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_the_Moon" title="Exploration of the Moon">exploration of the Moon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Its motto is <i>One JAXA</i><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and its corporate slogan is <i>Explore to Realize</i> (formerly <i>Reaching for the skies, exploring space</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Japan%27s_space_development" title="Japan&#39;s space development">Japan\'s space development</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Experiment_Module_Kibo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Japanese_Experiment_Module_Kibo.jpg/200px-Japanese_Experiment_Module_Kibo.jpg" width="200" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Japanese_Experiment_Module_Kibo.jpg/300px-Japanese_Experiment_Module_Kibo.jpg 1.5x, class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Experiment_Module_Kibo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>JAXA <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Experiment Module"><i>Kibo</i></a>, the largest module of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>On 1 October 2003, three organizations were merged to form the new JAXA: Japan\'s <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Space_and_Astronautical_Science" title="Institute of Space and Astronautical Science">Institute of Space and Astronautical Science</a> (ISAS), the <a href="/wiki/National_Aerospace_Laboratory_of_Japan" title="National Aerospace Laboratory of Japan">National Aerospace Laboratory of Japan</a> (NAL), and <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Development_Agency_of_Japan" title="National Space Development Agency of Japan">National Space Development Agency of Japan</a> (NASDA). JAXA was formed as an <a href="/wiki/Independent_Administrative_Institution" title="Independent Administrative Institution">Independent Administrative Institution</a> administered by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education,_Culture,_Sports,_Science_and_Technology" title="Ministry of Education, Culture, Sports, Science and Technology">Ministry of Education, Culture, Sports, Science and Technology</a> (MEXT) and the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Internal_Affairs_and_Communications" title="Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications">Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications</a> (MIC).<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before the merger, ISAS was responsible for <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a> and planetary research, while NAL was focused on aviation research. NASDA, which was founded on 1 October 1969, had developed <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rockets</a>, satellites, and also built the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Experiment Module">Japanese Experiment Module</a>. The old NASDA headquarters were located at the current site of the <a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">Tanegashima Space Center</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Tanegashima" title="Tanegashima">Tanegashima Island</a>, 115 kilometers south of <a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%ABsh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Ky\xc5\xabsh\xc5\xab">Ky\xc5\xabsh\xc5\xab</a>. NASDA also trained Japanese astronauts, who flew with the US <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Japan_Basic_Space_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Japan Basic Space Law">Basic Space Law</a> was passed in 2008, and the jurisdictional authority of JAXA moved from MEXT to the Strategic Headquarters for Space Development (SHSD) in the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Japan" title="Cabinet of Japan">Cabinet</a>, led by the Prime Minister.  In 2016, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Space_Policy_Secretariat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Space Policy Secretariat (page does not exist)">National Space Policy Secretariat</a> (NSPS) was set up the Cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-spacetechasia20180814_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spacetechasia20180814-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, new legislation extended JAXA\'s remit from peaceful purposes only to include some military space development, such as missile early warning systems. Political control of JAXA passed from MEXT to the Prime Minister\'s <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Office_(Japan)" title="Cabinet Office (Japan)">Cabinet Office</a> through a new Space Strategy Office.<sup id="cite_ref-defensenews-20120622_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-defensenews-20120622-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JAXA_Head_office.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/JAXA_Head_office.JPG/200px-JAXA_Head_office.JPG" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/JAXA_Head_office.JPG/300px-JAXA_Head_office.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/JAXA_Head_office.JPG/400px-JAXA_Head_office.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JAXA_Head_office.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head Office</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg/200px-H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg/300px-H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg/400px-H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:H-IIA_F13_launching_KAGUYA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tanegashima Space Center</div></div></div>\n<p>JAXA is composed of the following organizations:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Space Technology Directorate I</li>\n<li>Space Technology Directorate II</li>\n<li>Human Spaceflight Technology Directorate</li>\n<li>Research and Development Directorate</li>\n<li>Aeronautical Technology Directorate</li>\n<li>Institute of Space and Astronautical Science (ISAS)</li>\n<li>Space Exploration Innovation Hub Center</li></ul>\n<p>JAXA has research centers in many locations in Japan, and some offices overseas. Its headquarters are in <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dfu,_Tokyo" title="Ch\xc5\x8dfu, Tokyo">Ch\xc5\x8dfu, Tokyo</a>. It also has\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth_Observation_Research_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Earth Observation Research Center (page does not exist)">Earth Observation Research Center</a> (EORC), Tokyo</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Earth_Observation_Center_(Japan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth Observation Center (Japan)">Earth Observation Center</a> (EOC) in <a href="/wiki/Hatoyama,_Saitama" title="Hatoyama, Saitama">Hatoyama, Saitama</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Noshiro_Testing_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Noshiro Testing Center">Noshiro Testing Center</a> (NTC) in <a href="/wiki/Noshiro,_Akita" title="Noshiro, Akita">Noshiro, Akita</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Established in 1962. It carries out development and testing of rocket engines.</li>\n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sanriku_Balloon_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sanriku Balloon Center (page does not exist)">Sanriku Balloon Center</a> (SBC) \xe2\x80\x93 Balloons have been launched from this site since 1971.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kakuda_Space_Center" title="Kakuda Space Center">Kakuda Space Center</a> (KSPC) in <a href="/wiki/Kakuda,_Miyagi" title="Kakuda, Miyagi">Kakuda, Miyagi</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Leads the development of rocket engines. Works mainly with development of liquid fuel engines.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sagamihara_Campus" title="Sagamihara Campus">Sagamihara Campus</a> (ISAS) \xe2\x80\x93 Development of experimental equipment for rockets and satellites. Also administrative buildings.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">Tanegashima Space Center</a> \xe2\x80\x93 currently the launch site for the <a href="/wiki/H-IIA" title="H-IIA">H-IIA</a> and <a href="/wiki/H-IIB" title="H-IIB">H-IIB</a> rockets.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tsukuba_Space_Center" title="Tsukuba Space Center">Tsukuba Space Center</a> (TKSC) in <a href="/wiki/Tsukuba" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsukuba">Tsukuba</a>. This is the center of Japan\'s space network. It is involved in research and development of satellites and rockets, and tracking and controlling of satellites. It  develops experimental equipment for the Japanese Experiment Module ("Kibo"). Training of astronauts also takes place here. For <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> operations, the Japanese Flight Control Team is located at the Space Station Integration &amp; Promotion Center (SSIPC) in Tsukuba. SSIPC communicates regularly with ISS crewmembers via S-band audio.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Uchinoura_Space_Center" title="Uchinoura Space Center">Uchinoura Space Center</a> \xe2\x80\x93 currently the launch site for the <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_(rocket)" title="Epsilon (rocket)">Epsilon rocket</a>.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rockets">Rockets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rockets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>JAXA uses the <a href="/wiki/H-IIA" title="H-IIA">H-IIA</a> (H "two" A) rocket from the former NASDA body and its variant  <a href="/wiki/H-IIB" title="H-IIB">H-IIB</a> to launch engineering test satellites, weather satellites, etc. For science missions like <a href="/wiki/X-ray_astronomy" title="X-ray astronomy">X-ray astronomy</a>, JAXA uses the <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_(rocket)" title="Epsilon (rocket)">Epsilon</a> rocket. For experiments in the upper atmosphere JAXA uses the SS-520, <a href="/wiki/S-520" class="mw-redirect" title="S-520">S-520</a>, and <a href="/wiki/S-310" class="mw-redirect" title="S-310">S-310</a> <a href="/wiki/Sounding_rocket" title="Sounding rocket">sounding rockets</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Successes">Successes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Successes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Prior to the establishment of JAXA, ISAS had been most successful in its space program in the field of X-ray astronomy during the 1980s and 1990s. Another successful area for Japan  has been <a href="/wiki/Very_Long_Baseline_Interferometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Very Long Baseline Interferometry">Very Long Baseline Interferometry</a> (VLBI) with the <a href="/wiki/HALCA" title="HALCA">HALCA</a> mission. Additional success was achieved with solar observation and research of the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, among other areas.\n</p><p>NASDA was mostly active in the field of communication satellite technology. However, since the satellite market of Japan is completely open, the first time a Japanese company won a contract for a civilian communication satellite was in 2005. Another prime focus of the NASDA body is Earth <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> observation.\n</p><p>JAXA was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Space_Foundation" title="Space Foundation">Space Foundation</a>\'s John L. "Jack" Swigert, Jr., Award for Space Exploration in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_development_and_missions">Launch development and missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Launch development and missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:H-II_series.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/H-II_series.png/180px-H-II_series.png" width="180" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/H-II_series.png/270px-H-II_series.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/H-II_series.png/360px-H-II_series.png 2x" data-file-width="644" data-file-height="908" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:H-II_series.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>H-IIA &amp; H-IIB</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rocket_history">Rocket history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rocket history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Japan launched its first satellite, <a href="/wiki/Ohsumi_(satellite)" title="Ohsumi (satellite)">Ohsumi</a>, in 1970, using ISAS\' <a href="/wiki/Lambda_(rocket)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lambda (rocket)">L-4S</a> rocket. Prior to the merger, ISAS used small solid-fueled launch vehicles, while NASDA developed larger liquid-fueled launchers. In the beginning, NASDA used licensed American models. The first model of liquid-fuelled launch vehicle indigenously developed in Japan was the <a href="/wiki/H-II" title="H-II">H-II</a>, introduced in 1994. However, at the end of the 1990s, with two H-II launch failures, Japanese rocket technology began to face criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_H-IIA_missions">Early H-IIA missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early H-IIA missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Japan\'s first space mission under JAXA, an H-IIA rocket launch on 29 November 2003, ended in failure due to stress problems. After a 15-month hiatus, JAXA performed a successful launch of an H-IIA rocket from <a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">Tanegashima Space Center</a>, placing a satellite into orbit on 26 February 2005.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_SS-520_mission">Orbital SS-520 mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Orbital SS-520 mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In January 2017, JAXA attempted and failed to put a mini satellite into orbit atop one of its SS520 series rockets.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A second attempt on February 2, 2018 was successful, putting a 10-pound CubeSat into Earth orbit. The rocket, known as the SS-520-5, is the world\'s smallest orbital launcher.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_and_interplanetary_missions">Lunar and interplanetary missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lunar and interplanetary missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Japan\'s first missions beyond Earth orbit were the 1985 <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_comet" class="mw-redirect" title="Halley&#39;s comet">Halley\'s comet</a> observation satellites <a href="/wiki/Sakigake" title="Sakigake">Sakigake</a> (MS-T5) and <a href="/wiki/Suisei_probe" class="mw-redirect" title="Suisei probe">Suisei</a> (PLANET-A). To prepare for future missions, ISAS tested Earth swing by orbits with the <a href="/wiki/Hiten" title="Hiten">Hiten</a> mission in 1990. The first Japanese interplanetary mission was the Mars Orbiter <a href="/wiki/Nozomi_(probe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nozomi (probe)">Nozomi</a> (PLANET-B), which was launched in 1998. It passed Mars in 2003, but failed to reach Mars orbit due to maneuvering systems failures earlier in the mission. Currently interplanetary missions remain at the ISAS group under the JAXA umbrella. However, for FY 2008 JAXA is planning to set up an independent working group within the organization. New head for this group will be <a href="/wiki/Hayabusa" title="Hayabusa">Hayabusa</a> project manager Kawaguchi.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (October 2018)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p><b>Active Missions:</b> <a href="/wiki/PLANET-C" class="mw-redirect" title="PLANET-C">PLANET-C</a>, <a href="/wiki/IKAROS" title="IKAROS">IKAROS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hayabusa_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Hayabusa 2">Hayabusa 2</a>  <b> Under Development:</b> <a href="/wiki/Smart_Lander_for_Investigating_Moon" title="Smart Lander for Investigating Moon">SLIM</a> <b>Retired:</b> <a href="/wiki/PLANET-B" class="mw-redirect" title="PLANET-B">PLANET-B</a>, <a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">SELENE</a>, <a href="/wiki/MUSES-C" class="mw-redirect" title="MUSES-C">MUSES-C</a> <b>Cancelled:</b> <a href="/wiki/LUNAR-A" class="mw-redirect" title="LUNAR-A">LUNAR-A</a>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Small_body_exploration:_Hayabusa_mission">Small body exploration: Hayabusa mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Small body exploration: Hayabusa mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hayabusa_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Hayabusa 2">Hayabusa 2</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_hover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Hayabusa_hover.jpg/220px-Hayabusa_hover.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Hayabusa_hover.jpg/330px-Hayabusa_hover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Hayabusa_hover.jpg/440px-Hayabusa_hover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="970" data-file-height="653" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_hover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hayabusa" title="Hayabusa">Hayabusa</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On 9 May 2003, <a href="/wiki/Hayabusa" title="Hayabusa">Hayabusa</a> (meaning, <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">Peregrine falcon</a>), was launched from an <a href="/wiki/M-V" title="M-V">M-V</a> rocket.  The goal of the mission was to collect samples from a small <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" class="mw-redirect" title="Near-Earth asteroid">near-Earth asteroid</a> named <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa">25143 Itokawa</a>. The craft <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">rendezvoused</a> with the asteroid in September 2005. It was confirmed that the spacecraft successfully landed on the asteroid in November 2005, after some initial confusion regarding the incoming data. Hayabusa returned to Earth with samples from the asteroid on 13 June 2010.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_explorations">Lunar explorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Lunar explorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After <a href="/wiki/Hiten" title="Hiten">Hiten</a> in 1990, ISAS planned a lunar penetrator mission called <a href="/wiki/LUNAR-A" class="mw-redirect" title="LUNAR-A">LUNAR-A</a> but after delays due to technical problems, the project was terminated in January 2007. The seismometer penetrator design for LUNAR-A may be reused in a future mission.\n</p><p>On 14 September 2007, JAXA succeeded in launching the lunar orbit explorer <i><a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">Kaguya</a></i>, also known as SELENE (costing 55 billion yen including launch vehicle), the largest such mission since the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, on an <a href="/wiki/H-2A" class="mw-redirect" title="H-2A">H-2A</a> rocket. Its mission is to gather data on the <a href="/wiki/Moon#Origin_and_geologic_evolution" title="Moon">moon\'s origin and evolution</a>.  It entered lunar orbit on 4 October 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> After 1 year and 8 months it impacted the lunar surface on 10 June 2009 at 18:25 UTC.\n</p><p>JAXA plans to launch its first lunar surface mission, <a href="/wiki/Smart_Lander_for_Investigating_Moon" title="Smart Lander for Investigating Moon">SLIM</a> (Smart Lander for Investigating Moon) on an <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_rocket" class="mw-redirect" title="Epsilon rocket">Epsilon rocket</a> in fiscal year 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_exploration">Planetary exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Planetary exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Japan\'s planetary missions have so far been limited to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System#Inner_Solar_System" title="Solar System">inner Solar System</a>, and emphasis has been put on magnetospheric and atmospheric research. The <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Nozomi_(spacecraft)" title="Nozomi (spacecraft)">Nozomi</a> (PLANET-B), which ISAS launched prior to the merger of the three aerospace institutes, became one of the earliest difficulties the newly formed JAXA faced. Nozomi ultimately passed 1,000&#160;km from the surface of Mars. On 20 May 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> Climate Orbiter <a href="/wiki/Akatsuki_(spacecraft)" title="Akatsuki (spacecraft)">Akatsuki</a> (PLANET-C) and <a href="/wiki/IKAROS" title="IKAROS">IKAROS</a> solar sail demonstrator was launched by a <a href="/wiki/H-2A" class="mw-redirect" title="H-2A">H-2A</a> launch vehicle.\n</p><p>On 7 December 2010, Akatsuki was unable to complete its Venus orbit insertion maneuver. Akatsuki finally entered Venus orbit in 7 December 2015, making it the first Japanese spacecraft to orbit another planet, sixteen years after the originally planned orbital insertion of Nozomi. One of Akatsuki\'s main goal is to uncover the mechanism behind Venus atmosphere\'s <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus#Circulation" title="Atmosphere of Venus">super-rotation</a>, a phenomenon in which the cloud top winds in the troposphere circulates around the planet faster than the speed that Venus itself rotates. A thorough explanation for this phenomenon has yet been found.\n</p><p>JAXA/ISAS was part of the international <a href="/wiki/Europa_Jupiter_System_Mission_%E2%80%93_Laplace" title="Europa Jupiter System Mission \xe2\x80\x93 Laplace">Laplace</a> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> mission proposal from its foundation. A Japanese contribution was sought in the form of an independent orbiter to research Jupiter\'s magnetosphere, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Magnetospheric_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Magnetospheric Orbiter">JMO</a> (Jupiter Magnetospheric Orbiter). Although JMO never left the conception phase, ISAS scientists will see their instruments reaching Jupiter on the ESA-led <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moon_Explorer" class="mw-redirect" title="Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer">JUICE</a> (Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer) mission. JUICE is a reformulation of the ESA <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> orbiter from the Laplace project. JAXA\'s contribution includes providing components of the RPWI (Radio &amp; Plasma Wave Investigation), PEP (Particle Environment Package), GALA (GAnymede Laser Altimeter) instruments.\n</p><p>JAXA is reviewing a new spacecraft mission to the Martian system; a sample return mission to <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> called <a href="/wiki/Martian_Moons_Explorer" class="mw-redirect" title="Martian Moons Explorer">MMX</a> (Martian Moons Explorer).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> First revealed in 9 June 2015, MMX\'s primary goal is to determine the origin of the <a href="/wiki/Martian_moons" class="mw-redirect" title="Martian moons">Martian moons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Alongside collecting samples from Phobos, MMX will perform <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a>, and may also observe the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere of Mars</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> As of January 2016, MMX is to be launched in fiscal year 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/BepiColombo" title="BepiColombo">BepiColombo</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_sail_research">Solar sail research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Solar sail research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 9 August 2004, ISAS successfully deployed two prototype <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">solar sails</a> from a sounding rocket. A clover type sail was deployed at 122&#160;km altitude and a fan type sail was deployed at 169&#160;km altitude. Both sails used 7.5 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometer</a> thick film.\n</p><p>ISAS tested a solar sail again as a sub payload to the <a href="/wiki/Akari_(satellite)" title="Akari (satellite)">Akari</a> (ASTRO-F) mission on 22 February 2006. However the solar sail did not deploy fully.  ISAS tested a solar sail again as a sub payload of the <a href="/wiki/Hinode" title="Hinode">SOLAR-B</a> launch at 23 September 2006, but contact with the probe was lost. The <a href="/wiki/IKAROS" title="IKAROS">IKAROS</a> solar sail was launched on 21 May 2010. The solar sail deployed successfully. The goal is to have a solar sail mission to Jupiter after 2020.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy_program">Astronomy program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Astronomy program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Scientific_research_on_the_ISS" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific research on the ISS">Scientific research on the ISS</a></div>\n<p>The first Japanese astronomy mission was x-ray satellite Hakucho (Corsa-B), which was launched in 1979. Later ISAS moved into solar observation, radio astronomy through Space <a href="/wiki/VLBI" class="mw-redirect" title="VLBI">VLBI</a> and infrared astronomy. Active missions are <a href="/wiki/Hinode" title="Hinode">SOLAR-B</a>, <a href="/wiki/MAXI_(ISS_Experiment)" title="MAXI (ISS Experiment)">MAXI</a>, <a href="/wiki/SPRINT-A" class="mw-redirect" title="SPRINT-A">SPRINT-A</a>, and <a href="/wiki/CALET" class="mw-redirect" title="CALET">CALET</a>. Missions under development are <a href="/wiki/X-Ray_Imaging_and_Spectroscopy_Mission" title="X-Ray Imaging and Spectroscopy Mission">XRISM</a>. Retired missions are <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-F" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-F">ASTRO-F</a>, <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-EII" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-EII">ASTRO-EII</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-H" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-H">ASTRO-H</a>. <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-G" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-G">ASTRO-G</a> is a cancelled mission.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrared_astronomy">Infrared astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Infrared astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astro-E_clean-room.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Astro-E_clean-room.jpg/220px-Astro-E_clean-room.jpg" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Astro-E_clean-room.jpg/330px-Astro-E_clean-room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Astro-E_clean-room.jpg/440px-Astro-E_clean-room.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1180" data-file-height="1703" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astro-E_clean-room.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>ASTRO-E</div></div></div>\n<p>Japan\'s first infrared astronomy mission was the 15&#160;cm <a href="/wiki/Infrared_Telescope_in_Space" class="mw-redirect" title="Infrared Telescope in Space">IRTS</a> telescope which was part of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Flyer_Unit" title="Space Flyer Unit">SFU</a> multipurpose satellite in 1995. IRTS scanned during its one-month lifetime around 7% of the sky before SFU got brought back to Earth by the Space Shuttle. During the 1990s JAXA also gave ground support for the <a href="/wiki/ESA" class="mw-redirect" title="ESA">ESA</a> <a href="/wiki/Infrared_Space_Observatory" title="Infrared Space Observatory">Infrared Space Observatory</a> (ISO) infrared mission.\n</p><p>The next step for JAXA was the <a href="/wiki/Akari_(satellite)" title="Akari (satellite)">Akari</a> spacecraft, with the pre-launch designation <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-F" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-F">ASTRO-F</a>. This satellite was launched on 21 February 2006. Its mission is <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> astronomy with a 68&#160;cm telescope.  This is the first all sky survey since the first infrared mission <a href="/wiki/IRAS" title="IRAS">IRAS</a> in 1983.\n(A 3.6&#160;kg <a href="/wiki/Nanosatellite" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanosatellite">nanosatellite</a> named <a href="/wiki/CUTE-1.7" class="mw-redirect" title="CUTE-1.7">CUTE-1.7</a> was also released from the same launch vehicle.)<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>JAXA is also doing further R&amp;D for increasing the performance of its mechanical coolers for its future infrared mission, <a href="/wiki/SPICA_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="SPICA spacecraft">SPICA</a>. This would enable a warm launch without liquid helium. SPICA has the same size as the ESA <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel Space Observatory</a> mission, but is planned to have a temperature of just 4.5 K and will be much colder. Unlike Akari, which had a <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">geocentric orbit</a>, SPICA will be located at Sun\xe2\x80\x93Earth <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L2" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>2</sub></a>. The launch is expected in 2027 or 2028 on JAXA\'s new <a href="/wiki/H3_Launch_Vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="H3 Launch Vehicle">H3 Launch Vehicle</a>, however the mission is not yet fully funded. ESA and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> may also each contribute an instrument.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="X-ray_astronomy">X-ray astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: X-ray astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-H" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-H">ASTRO-H</a></div>\n<p>Starting from 1979 with <a href="/wiki/Hakucho" title="Hakucho">Hakucho</a> (CORSA-b), for nearly two decades Japan had achieved continuous observation with its <a href="/wiki/Hinotori_(satellite)" title="Hinotori (satellite)">Hinotori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenma" title="Tenma">Tenma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ginga_(satellite)" title="Ginga (satellite)">Ginga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Satellite_for_Cosmology_and_Astrophysics" title="Advanced Satellite for Cosmology and Astrophysics">ASCA</a> (ASTRO-A through D) x-ray observation satellites. However, in the year 2000 the launch of Japan\'s fifth x-ray observation satellite, <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-E" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-E">ASTRO-E</a> failed (as it failed at launch it never received a proper name).\n</p><p>Then on 10 July 2005, JAXA was finally able to launch a new <a href="/wiki/X-ray_astronomy" title="X-ray astronomy">X-ray astronomy</a> mission named <a href="/wiki/Suzaku_(satellite)" title="Suzaku (satellite)">Suzaku</a> (ASTRO-EII). This launch was important for JAXA, because in the five years since the launch failure of the original ASTRO-E satellite, Japan was without an <a href="/wiki/X-ray_telescope" title="X-ray telescope">x-ray telescope</a>.  Three instruments were included in this satellite: an <a href="/wiki/X-ray_spectrometer" class="mw-redirect" title="X-ray spectrometer">X-ray spectrometer</a> (XRS), an <a href="/w/index.php?title=X-ray_imaging_spectrometer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="X-ray imaging spectrometer (page does not exist)">X-ray imaging spectrometer</a> (XIS), and a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_X-ray_detector&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hard X-ray detector (page does not exist)">hard X-ray detector</a> (HXD). However, the XRS was rendered inoperable due to a malfunction which caused the satellite to lose its supply of liquid helium.\n</p><p>The next JAXA x-ray mission is the <a href="/wiki/MAXI_(ISS_Experiment)" title="MAXI (ISS Experiment)">Monitor of All-sky X-ray Image (MAXI)</a>. MAXI continuously monitors astronomical X-ray objects over a broad energy band (0.5 to 30 keV). MAXI is installed on the Japanese external module of the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> On 17 February 2016, <a href="/wiki/Hitomi_(satellite)" title="Hitomi (satellite)">Hitomi</a> (ASTRO-H) was launched as the successor to Suzaku, which completed its mission a year before.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_observation">Solar observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Solar observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Japan\'s solar astronomy started in the early 80s with the launch of the <i><a href="/wiki/Hinotori_(satellite)" title="Hinotori (satellite)">Hinotori</a></i> (ASTRO-A) x-ray mission.  The <a href="/wiki/Hinode" title="Hinode">Hinode</a> (SOLAR-B) spacecraft, the follow-on to the joint Japan/US/UK <a href="/wiki/Yohkoh" title="Yohkoh">Yohkoh</a> (SOLAR-A) spacecraft, was launched on 23 September 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> A SOLAR-C can be expected sometime after 2020. However no details are worked out yet other than it will not be launched with the former ISAS\'s Mu rockets. Instead a H-2A from Tanegashima could launch it. As H-2A is more powerful, SOLAR-C could either be heavier or be stationed at <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L1" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>1</sub></a> (<a href="/wiki/Lagrange_point" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagrange point">Lagrange point</a> 1).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_astronomy">Radio astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Radio astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1998 Japan launched the HALCA (MUSES-B) Mission, the world\'s first spacecraft dedicated to conduct SPACE VLBI observations of pulsars, among others.  To do so, ISAS set up a ground network around the world through international cooperation.  The observation part of the mission lasted until 2003 and the satellite was retired at the end of 2005. In FY 2006 Japan funded the <a href="/wiki/ASTRO-G" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTRO-G">ASTRO-G</a> as the succeeding mission.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Communication.2C_positioning_and_technology_tests"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication,_positioning_and_technology_tests">Communication, positioning and technology tests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Communication, positioning and technology tests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>One of the primary duties of the former NASDA body was the testing of new space technologies, mostly in the field of communication. The first test satellite was ETS-I, launched in 1975. However, during the 1990s NASDA was hit by bad luck with the problems surrounding the ETS-VI and COMETS missions.\n</p><p>In February 2018, JAXA announced a research collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> to test a <a href="/wiki/Laser_communication_in_space" title="Laser communication in space">laser communication</a> system from the Kibo module in late 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Testing of communication technologies remains to be one of JAXA\'s key duties in cooperation with <a href="/wiki/NICT" class="mw-redirect" title="NICT">NICT</a>.\n</p><p><b>Active Missions:</b> <a href="/wiki/Reimei" title="Reimei">INDEX</a>, <a href="/wiki/WINDS" title="WINDS">WINDS</a>, <a href="/wiki/QZS-1" class="mw-redirect" title="QZS-1">QZS-1</a> <b>Under Development:</b> <a href="/wiki/SLATS" class="mw-redirect" title="SLATS">SLATS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quasi-Zenith_Satellite_System" title="Quasi-Zenith Satellite System">QZS-2</a>, QZS-3, QZS-4, ETS-IX <b>Retired:</b> <a href="/wiki/OICETS" title="OICETS">OICETS</a>, <a href="/wiki/ETS-VIII" title="ETS-VIII">ETS-VIII</a>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="i-Space_:_ETS-VIII.2C_WINDS_and_QZS-1"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="i-Space_:_ETS-VIII,_WINDS_and_QZS-1">i-Space&#160;: ETS-VIII, WINDS and QZS-1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: i-Space\xc2\xa0: ETS-VIII, WINDS and QZS-1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>To upgrade Japan\'s communication technology the Japanese state launched the i-Space initiative with the ETS-VIII and WINDS missions.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>ETS-VIII was launched on 18 December 2006. The purpose of <a href="/wiki/ETS-VIII" title="ETS-VIII">ETS-VIII</a> is to test communication equipment with two very large antennas and an atomic clock test. On 26 December both antennas were successfully deployed. This didn\'t come unexpected, since JAXA tested the deployment mechanism before with the LDREX-2 Mission, which was launched on 14 October with the European Ariane 5. The test was successful. The mission of <a href="/wiki/WINDS" title="WINDS">WINDS</a> (Kizuna) is to create the worlds fastest satellite internet connection. WINDS was launched in February 2008.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="OICETS_and_INDEX">OICETS and INDEX</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: OICETS and INDEX">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 24 August 2005, JAXA launched the experimental satellites <a href="/wiki/OICETS" title="OICETS">OICETS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reimei" title="Reimei">INDEX</a> on a Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Dnepr_(rocket)" title="Dnepr (rocket)">Dnepr rocket</a>. OICETS (Kirari) is a mission tasked with tesing optical links with the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA) <a href="/wiki/ARTEMIS" class="mw-redirect" title="ARTEMIS">ARTEMIS</a> satellite, which is around 40,000&#160;km away from OICETS. The experiment was successful on 9 December, when the link could be established. In March 2006 JAXA could establish with OICETS the worldwide first optical links between a LEO satellite and a ground station first in Japan and in June 2006 with a mobile station in Germany.\n</p><p>INDEX (Reimei) is a small 70&#160;kg satellite for testing various equipment, and functions as an <a href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurora</a> observation mission as well. The Reimei satellite is currently in its extended mission phase.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_observation_programme">Earth observation programme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Earth observation programme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Japan\'s first Earth observation satellites were MOS-1a and MOS-1b launched in 1987 and 1990. During the 1990s and the new millennium this programme came under heavy fire, because both Adeos (Midori) and <a href="/wiki/Adeos_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Adeos 2">Adeos 2</a> (Midori 2) satellites failed after just 10 months in orbit.\n</p><p><b>Active Missions:</b> <a href="/wiki/GOSAT" class="mw-redirect" title="GOSAT">GOSAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/GCOM-W" class="mw-redirect" title="GCOM-W">GCOM-W</a>, <a href="/wiki/ALOS-2" title="ALOS-2">ALOS-2</a> <b>Under Development:</b> <a href="/wiki/GCOM-C" class="mw-redirect" title="GCOM-C">GCOM-C</a>, GOSAT-2, ALOS-3 <b>Retired:</b> <a href="/wiki/ALOS" class="mw-redirect" title="ALOS">ALOS</a>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ALOS">ALOS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: ALOS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MTSAT-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/MTSAT-1.jpg/220px-MTSAT-1.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/MTSAT-1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="214" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MTSAT-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>MTSAT-1</div></div></div>\n<p>In January 2006, JAXA successfully launched the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Land_Observation_Satellite" title="Advanced Land Observation Satellite">Advanced Land Observation Satellite</a> (ALOS/Daichi). Communication between ALOS and the ground station in Japan will be done through the Kodama Data Relay Satellite, which was launched during 2002. This project is under intense pressure due to the shorter than expected lifetime of the ADEOS II (Midori) Earth Observation Mission. For missions following Daichi, JAXA opted to separate it into a radar satellite (<a href="/wiki/ALOS-2" title="ALOS-2">ALOS-2</a>) and an optical satellite (ALOS-3). ALOS 2 SAR was launched in May 2014.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rainfall_observation">Rainfall observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Rainfall observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since Japan is an island nation and gets struck by typhoons every year, research about the dynamics of the atmosphere is a very important issue. For this reason Japan launched in 1997 the <a href="/wiki/TRMM" class="mw-redirect" title="TRMM">TRMM</a> (Tropical Rainfall Measuring Mission) satellite in cooperation with NASA, to observe the tropical rainfall seasons. For further research NASDA had launched the ADEOS and ADEOS II missions in 1996 and 2003. However, due to various reasons,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="what would those reasons be? (November 2017)">specify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> both satellites had a much shorter than expected life term.\n</p><p>On 28 February 2014, a H-2A rocket launched the <a href="/wiki/GPM_Core_Observatory" class="mw-redirect" title="GPM Core Observatory">GPM Core Observatory</a>, a satellite jointly developed by JAXA and NASA. The GPM mission is the successor to the TRMM mission, which by the time of the GPM launch had been noted as highly successful. JAXA provided the <a href="/wiki/Global_Precipitation_Measurement" title="Global Precipitation Measurement">Global Precipitation Measurement</a>/Dual-frequency Precipitation Radar (GPM/DPR) Instrument for this mission. Global Precipitation Measurement itself is a satellite constellation, whilst the GPM Core Observatory provides a new calibration standard for other satellites in the constellation. Other countries/agencies like France, India, ESA etc. provides the subsatellites. The aim of GPM is to measure global rainfall with unprecedented detail.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monitoring_of_carbon_dioxide">Monitoring of carbon dioxide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Monitoring of carbon dioxide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At the end of the 2008 fiscal year, JAXA launched the satellite <a href="/wiki/GOSAT" class="mw-redirect" title="GOSAT">GOSAT</a> (Greenhouse Gas Observing SATellite) to help scientists determine and monitor the density distribution of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.  The satellite is being jointly developed by JAXA and Japan\'s <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_the_Environment_(Japan)" title="Ministry of the Environment (Japan)">Ministry of the Environment</a>.  JAXA is building the satellite while the Ministry is in charge of the data that will be collected.  Since the number of ground-based carbon dioxide observatories cannot monitor enough of the world\'s atmosphere and are distributed unevenly throughout the globe, the GOSAT may be able to gather more accurate data and fill in the gaps on the globe where there are no observatories on the ground.  Sensors for <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gasses</a> are also being considered for the satellite, although the plans are not yet finalized.  The satellite weighs approximately 1650&#160;kg and is expected to have a life span of 5 years.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="GCOM_series">GCOM series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: GCOM series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Next funded earth observation mission after GOSAT is the GCOM (<a href="/wiki/Global_Change_Observation_Mission" title="Global Change Observation Mission">Global Change Observation Mission</a>) earth observation programme as a successor to ADEOS II (Midori) and the <a href="/wiki/Aqua_(satellite)" title="Aqua (satellite)">Aqua</a> mission. To reduce the risk and for a longer observation time the mission will be split into smaller satellites. Altogether GCOM will be a series of six satellites. The first satellite, <a href="/wiki/GCOM-W" class="mw-redirect" title="GCOM-W">GCOM-W</a> (Shizuku) was launched on 17 May 2012 with the H-IIA. The launch of the second satellite, <a href="/wiki/GCOM-C" class="mw-redirect" title="GCOM-C">GCOM-C</a> is currently planned for 2017.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellites_for_other_agencies">Satellites for other agencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Satellites for other agencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>For weather observation Japan launched in February 2005 the Multi-Functional Transport Satellite 1R (<a href="/wiki/MTSAT" class="mw-redirect" title="MTSAT">MTSAT-1R</a>). The success of this launch was critical for Japan, since the original MTSAT-1 couldn\'t be put into orbit because of a launch failure with the H-2 rocket in 1999. Since then Japan relied for weather forecasting on an old satellite which was already beyond its useful life term and on American systems.\n</p><p>On 18 February 2006, JAXA, as head of the H-IIA at this time, successfully launched the MTSAT-2 aboard a H-2A rocket. MTSAT-2 is the backup to the MTSAT-1R. The MTSAT-2 uses the DS-2000 satellite bus developed by Mitsubishi Electric.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>  The DS-2000 is also used for the DRTS Kodama, ETS-VIII and the Superbird 7 communication satellite, making it the first commercial success for Japan.\n</p><p>As a secondary mission both the MTSAT-1R and MTSAT-2 help to direct air traffic.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_JAXA_satellites_currently_in_use">Other JAXA satellites currently in use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other JAXA satellites currently in use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/GEOTAIL" class="mw-redirect" title="GEOTAIL">GEOTAIL</a> magnetosphere observation satellite (since 1992)</li>\n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=DRTS&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="DRTS (page does not exist)">DRTS</a> (Kodama) Data Relay Satellite, since 2002. (Projected Life Span is 7 years)</li></ul>\n<p>Ongoing joint missions with NASA are the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Rainfall_Measuring_Mission" title="Tropical Rainfall Measuring Mission">Tropical Rainfall Measuring Mission</a> (TRMM), the <a href="/wiki/Aqua_(satellite)" title="Aqua (satellite)">Aqua</a> Earth Observation Satellite, and the <a href="/wiki/Global_Precipitation_Measurement" title="Global Precipitation Measurement">Global Precipitation Measurement</a> (GPM) Core satellite.\n</p><p>On May 11, 2018 JAXA has launched the first outer-space object registered by <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>. Satellite 1KUNS-PF created by University of Nairobi was launched from the Japanese module of the International Space Station.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Completed_missions">Completed missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Completed missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Akebono_(satellite)" title="Akebono (satellite)">Akebono</a> Aurora Observation 1989\xe2\x80\x932015 (retired)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Akari_(satellite)" title="Akari (satellite)">Akari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared_astronomy" title="Infrared astronomy">Infrared astronomy</a> mission 2006\xe2\x80\x932011 (retired)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/OICETS" title="OICETS">OICETS</a>, Technology Demonstration 2005\xe2\x80\x932009 (retired)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">SELENE</a>, Moon probe 2007\xe2\x80\x932009 (retired)</li>\n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Micro_Lab_Sat_1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Micro Lab Sat 1 (page does not exist)">Micro Lab Sat 1</a>, Small engineering mission, launch 2002. (retired 27 September 2006)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/HALCA" title="HALCA">HALCA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_VLBI" class="mw-redirect" title="Space VLBI">Space VLBI</a> 1997\xe2\x80\x932005 (retired)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nozomi_(probe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nozomi (probe)">Nozomi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> Mission 1998\xe2\x80\x932003 (failed)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/MDS-1" title="MDS-1">MDS-1</a>, Technology Demonstration 2002\xe2\x80\x932003 (retired)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/ADEOS_2" class="mw-redirect" title="ADEOS 2">ADEOS 2</a>, (Midori 2) Earth Observation 2002\xe2\x80\x932003 (lost)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_missions">Future missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Future missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg/180px-HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg" width="180" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg/270px-HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg/360px-HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HTV-1_approaches_ISS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HTV-1</div></div></div>\n<p>As JAXA shifted away from international efforts beginning in 2005, plans are developing for independent space missions, such as a proposed manned mission to the moon.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2009_and_beyond">2009 and beyond</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 2009 and beyond">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <small><i>(November 2010)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>On 23 February 2008 JAXA launched the Wideband InterNetworking engineering test and Demonstration Satellite (<a href="/wiki/WINDS" title="WINDS">WINDS</a>), also called "KIZUNA." WINDS will facilitate experiments with faster internet connections. The launch, using <a href="/wiki/H-IIA" title="H-IIA">H-IIA</a> launch vehicle 14, took place from the <a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">Tanegashima Space Center</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 10 September 2009 the first <a href="/wiki/H-IIB" title="H-IIB">H-IIB</a> rocket was successfully launched, delivering the <a href="/wiki/HTV-1" title="HTV-1">HTV-1</a> freighter to resupply the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the year 2009 JAXA plans to launch the first satellite of the <a href="/wiki/Quasi_Zenith_Satellite_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Quasi Zenith Satellite System">Quasi Zenith Satellite System</a> (QZSS), a subsystem of the global positioning system (GPS). Two others are expected to follow later. If successful, one satellite will be in a zenith position over Japan full-time. The QZSS mission is the last scheduled major independent mission for JAXA, as no major civilian projects were funded after that for now. The only exception is the IGS programme which will be continued beyond 2008. However it seems Japan is pressing forward now with the GCOM earth observation satellites as successors to the ADEOS missions. First launch is planned for 2010. In 2009 Japan also plans to launch a new version of the IGS with an improved resolution of 60&#160;cm.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_schedule">Launch schedule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Launch schedule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The maiden flight of the <a href="/wiki/H-IIB" title="H-IIB">H-IIB</a> and the HTV occurred in 1 September 2009. After the first flight, one HTV launch is scheduled during each FY until 2019. (If not mentioned otherwise launch vehicle for the following missions is the <a href="/wiki/H-IIA" title="H-IIA">H-IIA</a>.)\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="FY_2016">FY 2016</span><span dioxide monitor mission<sup id="cite_ref-kihon151208_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kihon151208-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Quasi-Zenith_Satellite_System" id="cite_ref-kihon151208_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kihon151208-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>QZS-4<sup id="cite_ref-kihon151208_34-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kihon151208-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="FY_2018">FY 2018</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="BepiColombo">BepiColombo</a>, joint ESA mission to <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, launch: Oct, 2018 (LV: Ariane 5)</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="FY_2019">FY 2019</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JAXA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: FY 2019">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<ul><li>Optical Data Relay satellite<sup id="cite_ref-kihon151208_34-7" class="reference"><a
